The Board of Supervisors by a unani
mous vote decided yesterday to override
llayor Schmitz's veto of thirty-two spe
cific items in the next tax budget where
by he proposed to save the sum of $134.
709 to be devoted to public improvements.
In taking this action the board allows
the budget as originally adopted to be
come a law, and thus renders the Mayor's
pruning efforts of no effect.
As a result of the board's action the
Health Department will continue the em
ployment of a large number of subordi
nates and the Police Department will be
furnished with several extra men which
the Mayor's veto dispensed with. Agaii^,
the Board of Public Works will revel in
the employment of many underlings who
had been officially decapitated by the
flavor's veto and a number of salaries
will be increased as originally proposed.
In refusing to accept the Mayor's veto
of the various items of the budget which
he thought unnecessary the Board of Su
pervisors intended no disrespect to the
chief magistrate, some of the members
say, but they felt that they had been
treated shabbily in having some of their
ewi pet appropriations lopped off. Each
Supervisor had some grievance on that
ex-oj-c,^ some friend who would lose his
potion 6hou;"d the Mayor's veto Â»tairj.
Even the members of his own party-
Lou ghery. Lynch. McClellan and Wynnâ
were in that quandary and voted against
their chief.
The statement made by Dr. Williamson,
president of the Health Board, that the
I.Iayor's veto had in the main affected
only civil service employes had some
weight. Again, the Supervisors believed,
eo some of them Fay, that as the Mayor
had sat with them during their delibera
tions as a committee of the whole to con-
Eider the budget he was well aware of
what was being done and his objections
should have been noted then. Once when
sn increased appropriation was with
cLrawn after the Mayor intimated that
he would strike it out the inference was
crawn that the Items already adopted
were acceptable to his Honor.
KAYOS FEELS REBUKE.
That the Mayor felt the rebuff keenly
was plain to be seen. He had expected
that a vote would be taken on each item
end not on the budget as a whole. He
had fond hopes that the board would find
it impossible to muster the necessary fif
teen votes to override his veto and was
visibly disappointed when the odds proved
overwhelmingly against him.
Before a vote was taken on the Mayor's
veto permission was granted to the heads
cf various departments affected thereby
to be heard regarding the effect of abol
ishing positions under their supervision.
Assessor Dodge made a plea that the
Items of 5G000 for additional clerks, $500
for stationery and $2500 for maps for his
effice be restored to the budget.
Dr. Williamson, president of the Health
Board, read a statement In which he said
that with two exceptions the positions
abolished by the Mayor's veto were unier
civil service. Williamson held that if, as
the Mayor had implied, the men were in
competent, their incompetency should
have been reported to the Civil Service
Commission. Referring to the Mayor's
recommendation that the Health Officer
devote the hours between 8:30 and 5 p. m.
to his official work, Williamson said that
official's duties could not be circumscribed
by the limitations of official hours, as the
health officer was often required, to do
city work at night. .'- Â¦ â¢' '} . ;

Chief bf Police Wittman pleaded for the

Xlayor, saying that the peace of the city

LONDON. June 23.â It is coronation,
nothing but coronation in London to-day.
From the highest to the lowest, from the
uignified Peer who walked to his club
jostled, elbowed, shoved by an eager,
pushing crowd of sight-seers, to " 'Appy
'Arry" from the East End, who did mos;
cf the shovir.g and uttered the most pic
turesque ejaculations, all talked of the
coronation and the arrival of the. King.
His Majesty arrived from Windsor ac
cording to arrangements and drove in
semi-fctate to Buckingham Palace. Â¦ He
wiij loudly cheered by the great crowds
which lined the streets.
Notwithstanding the optimistic reports
to the effect that - the physicians were
satisfied with the condition of the King's
health, it was noticed that his Majesty,
when he stepped from the train and walk
ed to his carriage, leaned rather heavily
ou his cane and moved as though he
suffered pain in the small of " his bacK.
Many of those who saw him were struck
by his tired expression and pale com
plexion. - : ,;:.
One of the most interesting events of
to-day was the rehearsal of the cprona
tion ceremony In Westminster Abbey. The
"robing hall," as the new temporary erec
tion is called, was simply crowded. AH
round were drawn up men belonging to
the King's company of Grenadier Guards.
At one end Lord Edmund Talbot was sur
rounded by all his gold staff of officers.
Altogether nearly I2u were sent off under
different commanders to various places
they are to take on the great day, when
they, will act as "pew openers," as they
are facetiously called.
TWO GREAT PROCESSIONS.
\\ hen all the gold staff officers were in
tbeir places the Duke of Norfolk and va
rious heralds marshaled the two great
processions of the King and the Queen.
The King's organist was presiding at the
organ and all the boys of Westminster, In
scarlet cossacks. were stationed in their
l^roper places ready to rehearse their
shouts of acclamation. Although the pro
cessions were extremely dignified, it was
rather amusing to see the many great
people concerned wearing make-believe
trains,
While the procession was forming the
four Duchesses had rehearsals of carrying
the golden canopy. The Duchess of Port
land was wearing black lace over whit"
silk. With her walked the young Duchess
of Marlborough, who was in gray and had
entered the Abbey with her sons, little
Lord Blandford and Lord Ivor Spencer
Churchill.
Lady Mary Howard, sister of the Duke
of Norfolk,, represented Queen Alexandra
on this occasion. She wore a long made
up train of the regulation length of twen
ty feet, which was carried by eight pages
Then came the supposed King's proces
sion. Sir Spencer Ponsonby Fane repre
sented his Majesty with a long brown
cloth train, and nearly all those who are
to be in the King's procession were pres
ent.
Among them was the Duke of Devon
shire, wearing his robes and coronet-
Duke of Somerset; TTukeoT Gfaftbn Â«who
yore his coronet with long, linen robe;
Lord Lucan; Lord Londonderry, carrying
the sword of state: Duke of Norfolk-
L'ord Shrewsbury, who only carried his
coronet and did not wear his robes; Lord
Grey de ftuthven. carrying â¢ his coronet
and an imaginary gold spur and Valking
with Lord Louden, who carried the other
spur, and the Duke of Abercorn wear
ing his robes and coronet. All were at
tended by their pages.
While the procession was very interest
ing, it sadly needed the supervision of a
good stage manager, for nobody seemed
quite to know what to do.
"The act of recognition" was the first
to be rehearsed. Sir Spencer Ponsonby
Fane, preceded by the Archbishop of Can
terbury and the Lord Chancellor, were
conducted to the four sides of the
'â¢theater," and the Archbishop said
"Sirs, we present unto you King Ed
ward, undoubted King of this realm
Wherefore all you who are come this day
to do your homage, are you willing to do
the game?" Whereupon everybody shout
ed out at the instigation of the Duke of

GAGE AND JUSTICE DOWNING.
Governor Takes Another Flight to
San Pedro to Gain Time.
SAN PEDRO, Cal., June 23.â Another
move was made here to-day by Governor
Henry T. Gage in his libel suit against
John D. Spreckels and W. S. Leake. The
Governor, accompanied by Attorney
Johnson Jones of the District Attorney's
office, came down from- Los Angeles this
morning and appeared before Justice
Henry C. Downing of Wilmington Town
ship, about 11 o'clock. An oral application
was made to Justice Downing to make
an order requiring the appearance of
Messrs. Spreckels and Leake before him
on some certain day for their arraignment
and examination^ .- Â¦"
It Is evidently the intention of Gage
and the District Attorney's office not to
wait for a decision from Judge Fritz In
San' Francisco, but to proceed without
regard to the case so far as Fritz's court
is concerned. Justice Downing was seen
i.M regard to the 1 matter., and when asked
as to the status of the application said
that he had the matter under advisement
r.nd that the District Attorney would be
in San Pedro to-morrow at 11:30 a. m., at
which, time he would -give his decision
so far as the application made to-day
was concerned. Continuing* he said:
â¢ "There is no question that the ap
plication will be granted to-morrow. It
is simply a matter of legal form. I will
order the defendants to appear for ar
raignment and examination." -
Justice Downing, when asked what date
he would- set for the arraignment and
ffXtmination, replied that he perhaps
would -order the appearance of Messrs.
Spreckels and Leake for next Monday,
June 30. Upon making a decision to-mor
row, the order will be placed In the hands
of Sheriff William Hammell of Los An
geles County, who will then proceed to
San Francisco.
His Clothes Are Nearly Burned Off
and tho Flesh Is Badly
Charred.
COLUMBIA. Mo., June 22.â The dead
body of Archibald Buckner, with the
clothes nearly burned off and the flesh
charred, has been found in the woods near
Rock Bridge. Evidence brought out at
the Coroner's Inquest shows that Buckner
was seen alive in company with David
Grant, drinking at the house of William
Moore. Grant left the house first, Buck
ner following.. Shortly afterward three
shots were heard. Grant returned to tho
house and asked for matches, saying he
had shot Buckner, but making no ex
planation. Search for the body was not
made until the next day. when the body
was found badly burned. Grant has dis
appeared.

Man of 70 Years, Wounded and
Without Food, Displays Re- .
i. markable Endurance.
PENDLETQN; Or., June 23.âThrough
out eighteen days and nights of torture,
with his head crushed by a fall, Joseph
Reyburn crawled, about helpless In the
wild stock country southwest of here.
Reyburn Is 70 years old, yet so wonderful
Is his endurance that he will recover.^Hls
is one of the most remarkable cases Of
hardihood ever recorded.
Reyburn . started to James Baxter's
ranch, fifteen miles from Shanlko, on
horseback. When half way there his sad
dle cinch broke, throwing him. His head
struck a rock. For forty-eight hours he
lay , unconscious; then, impelled by thirst,
he slowly dragged himself to Brush Can
yon, a half-mile distant, for water.
. For sixteen days the unfortunate cattle

MAYOR EXPRESSES REGRET.
Last night the Mayor issued the follow
ing statement regarding ther action of. the
Supervisors in overriding his veto mes
sage: â¢ - --":' .-*â¢',
The action of the Board of Supervisors In
overriding the thirty-two Hems that I vetoed
without giving due or any consideration to each
separate Item is a matter of sincere regret on
my part. My veto message was actuated solely
for the purpose of solving the problem of re
ducing the large salary roll of the various de
partments of the municipal government, and
the determination that I arrived at. was only
afte'r very careful study and investigation ex
tending over the entire period of my Incum
bency. It is a matter of sincere regret that the
Supervisors did not see fit to sustain me in my
veto of the different items of the budget, for I
tried and I believe I have treated every item
with fairness, and certainly did not expect
them to stand with the chronic office-holders.
. One of my pledges to the people before elec
tion was that. I would cut off all ornamental
salaries and have the niqney used therefor de
voted to public Improvements, and that I felt
sure that if I were elected I could save at le/Ist
$100,000 to the citizens of San Francisco by the
curtailing of unnecessary and overpaid em
ployes. Upon a clÂ«se examination . of the
budget and in listening to the arguments before
thg committee of the whole, I found that I
could do even more than I had promised,
namely, -to reduce the running expenses of the
city $100,000, and would. have succeeded in re
ducing the running expenses of the city gov
ernment $134,700, and with a sincere belief that
I was not impairing the efficiency of. any de
partment, ' and with the proper management
that the affairs of this municipality, could lje
run Just as successfully, and more so. than had
been done In the past two years.
I have carried out my pledge that I made to
the' people before election, and whether the Su
pervisors'in not upholding my action have car
ried out theirs I leave the public to decide.
This defeat In trying to save to the city $134,
700, like the ones I have sustained In the past
In trying to rid the city of Incompetent and
corrupt officials, will not deter me in the least
In further trying to do my duty as I see It, as
a man and as a servant of the. people.
The Supervisors must admit themselves 'that
the very last hours of their deliberation , was
simply a farce, and that the different Items of
the budget were carried over without that
careful deliberation and consideration which
this very Important question warranted. I took
up their work from where they left off, having
fully ten days longer to devote to it than they
had, and again I say in closing /that it is a
matter of extreme "resret on my part, and I
feel. sure on the part of the public at large that
the city should be deprived of the benefit of the
$134,700 that would have been saved to the tax;
payers of San. Francisco by sustaining the dif
ferent items that I vetoed.

Of the history of the Toppan case there
is much that the public will never know.
Ir would be impossible to tell the com
plete story of the life of the woman be
cause it would be laughed down as Im
probable and utterly foundatlonless. This
murderess had a passion for setting 1 fires,
as well as a greed for sacrificing human
lives. One instance showing her com
bined manias may be cited. She was em
ployed as a nurse in the family of a well
kiown lawyer of this State, but had no
opportunity to kill off her patients. When
the houee was comparatively deserted one
n?ght she deliberately set fire to one of
the rooms, hoping that the spread of the
flames might accomplish what she did not
dare otherwise attempt â the destruction
of a helpless and powerless womanâwith
out suspicion falling upon her. Fortunate
ly the fire was seen in time to save " the
patient. . .
Four of the murders to which she con
fessed have been made public by the
State officials, but the identity of the vic
tims of the other three is merely a matter
of conjecture. Inasmuch as she is sus
pected of fourteen murders, it 19 difficult
to learn to which seven crimes she has
confessed. The persons whom It is known
she has confessed to have killed are Mrs.
Mary D. Gibbons of Cataumet; Mrs. Geh
evieve D. Gordon and Allen P. Davis and
wife, the sister, father and mother of
Mrs. Gibbons. The reason that she gave
for killing her victims is too horrible to
Jane Toppan, prior to her trial, mads
a confession to the State authorities in
which she acknowledged the murder of
seven persons. She is suspected of other
murders.
Miss Toppan was ordered committed to
Taunton Insane Asylum for life. She will
be taken there to-morrow.

SAN RAFAEL, June 23.â Frank Haynes,
alias Miller, who 'killed Jerry Harris, a
fellow-convict, in San Quentin on March
13, was brought to trial to-day before Su
perior Judge Angellottl. The case of
Miller is a peculiar one, and the pro
ceedings will be exceptional in the history
of California's criminal trials. Miller,
though on trial for his life, is without
counsel, and vehemently protested when
Judge Angellotti sought to appoint an at
torney to represent him.
"When the case was called Judge Angel
lottl asked the defendant if he was ready
to proceed, but received no answer. He
asked a second ami a third time, where
upon Miller replied:
"I object to this trial proceeding."
"Well," said the court, "have you any
legal reasons to offer?"
No answer.
/'Uentlemen, we will proceed with the
case," said the court. "Mr. Miller, have
you an attorney?" â¢
Judge Angellotfl repeated this question
five times before Miller gave a negative
answer.
"If you desfre it the court will appoint
one," said Judge Angellotti. "In fact, I
did appoint "counsel and am still willing to
appoint any one. you desire."
To this Miller made no reply, whereupon
the court asked Attorney Hawkins to be
present during the trial and see that the
defendant had his legal rights at all
times. At this juncture Miller, half rising
fiom his chair and shaking- his linger at
Judge Angellotti, exclaimed:
"Mr. Hawkins will not dare touch the
case and you shall not allow him to
do so."
A recess of fifteen minutes was taken,
after which the trial proceeded. Judge An
gellotti, addressing the prisoner, said:
"If the defendant at any time desires
counsel this court will appoint one. Also,
if defendant wishes to excuse, any jurors,
he can do so."
The questioning of talesmen was begun
and finished within a half hour, Miller of
fering no objection or displaying any con
cern. A number 'of witnesses were exam
ined, telling the story of the prison
tragedy. The trial will continue^ to
morrow.
Just what defense the convict will offer
is not known. However, he has had sev
eral witnesses subpenaed from Lassen
County, including Judge Spencer.

San Jose's Oldest Native Dead.
SAN JOSE, June 23.â Mrs. Bernardino
Villagrana died to-day, aged 95 years. She
was born in San Jose and was the oldest
living native of the city. She was never
outside the county during her !ong life.
CORNING, June 23.â Corning was vis
ited this evening by a fire which swept
away almost all of the buildings in one
entire block. The blaze started in the
hay loft of Henley & Motz's livery stable.
In three minutes the structure was con
sumed, together with twenty horses, the
fire engine and all the vehicles. A stiff
wind from the west carried the flames
to the large barn belonging to R. A.'Fos
ter & Co., which rapidly burned and
spread the flames to the tankhouse of
Foster & Woodson's real estate office.
This was soon consumed, shutting off
the water supply and leaving the May
wood colony at. the mercy of the ele
ments. For some time it was feared that
the Hotel Maywood, just across the street
irom the Maywood colony office, would
suffer the same fate, but vigorous work
by a bucket brigade saved the structure.
The loss Is estimated at J10.000.

WEAVERVILLE, June 23.â An im
mense tusk of a pachyderm has been un
covered by men working the monitor of
the Trinity Gold Placer Mining Syndicate
of, London at its mines on Coffee Creek.
Superintendent . William Maitland says
the man who had charge of the, monitor,
called the piper, had gone forward to,ex
amine his bank and noticed a curious ob
ject protruding from the ground. He
supposed it was rock of some unusual
kind and picked up a piece for examina
tion. It was found to be very oily on the
surface and that considerable oil re
mained in the gravel bed. He summoned
assistance and an enormous tusk was un
covered. The point of the tusk was rest
ing on bedrock, the other end sticking up
through the gravel. All that was under
the ground was perfect, but badly de
cayedâso much so that it broke into
pieces "of its own weight when moved.
The recovered portion is six feet In
length and nine inches in diameter at the
thick end. The stream from the monitor
strikes with terrific force and doubtless
destroyed much, of the fossil before its
discovery.
The wireless telegraph system is about
to be reopened between Honolulu and the
islands of Maui and Hawaii, and recent
tests indicate that the system will work
very well.
There will be several years of litigation
before all the suits are settled, as some
fishing rights, held under the old Hawai
ian laws governing such grants and high
ly valued by the holders, are involved in
the suits.
As a result of the provision in the" act
of Congress providing a form of govern
ment for the Territory of Hawaii that
all claimants of fishing rights In the Ter
ritory should file their claims in the form
of suits against the Territory within two
years after the act went Into effect about
80 suits have been filed by parties claim
ing titles to fishing rights in various parts
of the islands. Most of the rights are
based upon old grants of the Hawaiian
monarchy and some of the titles date
back a long time, but they are all in ef
fect and exclude the public from sea
waters. v- ;

GRASS VALLEY, June 23.â News of
the miraculous escape from death of the
eight-j ear-old son of Rancher Orrin Per
rln reached Grass Valley to-day. Perrin,
in company with a cousin of the same
age, was on horseback looking for stray
ed cattle when he rode into an old min
ing shaft hidden from sight by bushes.
Horse and rider plunged to the bottom of
the shaft, which was partly filled with
water. By a miracle young Perrin re
tained his seat in the saddle or he would
have been dashed to death. His cries
brought his cousin to the edge of the
Fhaft, but he was unable to give assist
ance.

AUBURN. June 23.â J. D. Gill, who was
Injured in a mine near Georgetown a
few days ago, died on Saturday. He fell
down a shaft a distance of fourteen feet
and an iron tamping rod penetrated the
base of his skull, pressing upon the brain.
The doctors say it was phenomenal for a
man to live so long with such a wound.

BAKER CITY, Or., June 23.â A party of
fishermen returning from Rock' Creek to
day reported what might have been a
frightful disaster at Lake Kalamacus on
Friday .night. An irrigation company has
been engaged in boring a tunnel under
the bottom of the lake, a distance of
about 250 feet, so that the water could be
used during the dry months. The tunnel
was so planned that it would strike a
point about sixty feet below the bottom
of the lake. An uprise was started from
the end of the tunnel to tap the lake bot
tom, supposed to be sixty feet above.
Friday evening the workmen put in a
shot in the solid rock in the uprise.' With
a mighty roar the water of the lake broke
through into the tunnel, and just a mo
ment after the men gained, a place of
safety a stream of water six feet square
was spouting several hundred feet down
the canyon. Rock Creek at once became
a roaring torrent. Fortunately, there
were a lot of trees -in the lake, which
were uprooted- by the rushing water and
carried into the tunnel, thus stopping to
some extent the outflow. Had it not been
for this hindrance a mill and store build-
Ing on the creek below would have been
washed away and several farm, buildings
destroyed.
The lake is now empty and is useless as
a reservoir fo rthis season.

Word came from WaEhoe City that the
reported discoverer of the mine had
brought in a quantity of the rock and
that he had said that plenty more was in
sight. The party that was reported as
the discoverer is a man by the name of
Miles, and he has been In this section for
the past three months hunting over the
liills. especially in the Little Valley
country, where the hidden treasure is
supposed to be located. Others have been
in the search, and not one has ever re
turned with any ore that resembles Bark
ley's.
All sorts of stories as to the richness
of the rock are in the air, and some say

Two days ago word reached this city
that the Hobart sleuths had uncovered
the ledge. Immediately Barkley mounted
a horse and headed for the hills with a
rifle over his shoulder and a belt of car
tridges strapped around his waist. It is
said that if he discovered the rumor to
be true he will make a. strong 1 fight to
hold a Just share of the property he
opened to the world.
Soon after Barkley left San Francisco
detectives were dispatched to Nevada.
Among them was Thacker of Wells, Far-
Eo & Co. fame. Some of them shadowed
the engineer in the hope that he would
lead them to the treasure, while others
searched every portion of the Hobart
holdings to find whence the ore had be6n
dug. But the ledge had been carefully
covered, and whatever rock had been
taken therefrom had been worked by the
band process.
The Hobart heirs did not submit their
answer for some time. In the meantime
they attempted to locate the mine for
themselves, but, being' unsuccessful, of
fered Barkley 15 per cent of whatever he
might take from the claim. The discov
erer rejected the proposition, and, con
cealing his lind more carefully, sat down
to await developments. Barkley said he
would let the secret die with him rather
than accept such terms.
Barkley tried to keep his secret until he
obtained possession of the ground. He
visited San Francisco and tried to make
a purchase, but found that the news of
his discovery had reached the Hobart
heirs in some mysterious manner and he
Â¦was obliged to confess the facts, though
concealing the location of his mine from
them. Negotiations were then conducted,
which would permit Barkley to work the
property on a percentage proposition.

The story is one almost without prece
dent. The mine, which from samples of
ore taken from it rivals in wealth the
properties that made millionaires of some
of the argonauts, was discovered by Bud
Barkley, en engineer on the Virginia and
Truckee Railroad. When Barkley real
ized from the assays the richness of his
claim he searched the records to ascer
tain his title to the land and found it be
longed to the Hobart estate and was
secured by a United States patent. It is
timber land and runs a considerable
distance on the mountain side west of
Carson.

DAWSON, Y. T. (via Vancouver), June
22.â Charged with having received large
sums of money from American companies
for complicity in extensive frauds against
the Government two officials quit their
offices in Dawson to-day. They were D.
W. Davis, Collector of Customs for the
port of Dawson, and A. A. Cook, chief
clerk in Davis' department. On Saturday
they were suspended, and to-day they
slipped their resignations into the hands
of Chief Inspector McMichael, who had
gone north especially to investigate cus
toms leakages. It is alleged that sums
totaling $50,000, with incidental penalties,
are lacKing in the receipts of the office,
and that Davis accepted various sums
in connection with these frauds. McMi
chael spent little more than a week in
the examination of the books and out
side affairs, and then suspended the Col
lector until action could be taken in Ot
tawa. In the meantime E. S. Bushby has
been appointed to take the position of
Collector temporarily and to assist in
straightening out the affairs of the de
partment.
Officials of the Alaska Exploration
Company, doing a large trading and im
porting business in the Klondike, are
mentioned In the details of the alleged
frauds, and action may be taken against
them by criminal process for their share
in the reported supplying of money to
the officials.

BUTTE. Mont., June 23.â Thirty years
for. thirty dollars is the sentence given by
Judge McClarnen to-day to William Per
ry, vrho with A., C. Young, held up Tom
Donovan April 24. The sentence is . an
unusually, heavy one and causes the re
maining prisoners" in the county 'jail to
a^vait their trials in fear and trembling.
Judge McClarnen is the first Butte Judge
to give such a heavy sentence and he de
clares he will do all he can to rid Montana
of objectionable characters.
Thirty Years for Thirty Dollars.
De Armond of Missouri spoke at con
siderable length in opposition to the ad
ministration's Philippine policy. The other
speakers to-day were Olmsted of Pennsyl
vania, Williams of Illinois, Corliss of
Michigan and W. W. Kitchin pf North
Carolina. ; Â¦
Grosvenor in his speech also defended
the rules of the House against the crit
icism passed upon them and paid a high
tribute to Speaker Henderson's impartial
ity. .
. Debate on the Philippine civil govern
ment bill warmed up- in the House to
day and henceforth promises to be of a
much livelier character. The increased
-nterest is due to the injection into the
debate of a comparison of the cruelties
charged to have been practiced in the
Philippines with those which occurred
during the Civil War. The subject has
been lightly touched upon once or twice
before, but attracted little attention until
Grosvenor of . Ohio to-day revived the
memory of the extremities to which Grant
and Jackson were put during the Rebel
lion. This was followed late in the day
by a speech from Mahon of Pennsyl
vania, In which he paraded the horrors of
Andersonville and Llbby prisons. He pre
dicted that in the coming- elections the
American people would stand by the "boys
in blue."
During the greater part of the session
the unfinished business, the bill ratify
ing a convention with the Choctaw and
Chickasaw Indians, was under discussion.
It was not disposed of.
Beveridge of Indiana, chairman of the
committee, ' said, the measure had been
put over until next session by the com
mittee because it was not believed there
would be time now to consider it properly.
No action was taken on the motion.

SAN FRANCISCO. June . 23, 1002.
Notice is hereby given that outside bar (oft
end of kelp) whistling buoy* black and white
perpendicular stripes, marked . "S. D.," was
moved June 10 about 300 feet WSW. V, W.
from its former position. Coronado Hotel
tower NNE. % E.. Point Loma light N. %,W.
(westerly) 2\t, miles, right tangent of Point
Loma in range with fog bell house of Ballast
Point N. V4 W. Course N. % W. through
main channel between San Diego bar (outside)
buoy, black can No. 1, and Gammon shoal
buoy, red No. 2. Whistling buoy, bell buoy,
black can buoy No.' 1, "and inside bar buoy,
red No. 4. arc about In range N. % E.
This notice affects the List of Beacons and
Buoys, Pacific Coast. 1902. page 11.
By order of the Lighthouse Board.
J. B. MILTON, Commander. U. S. N.,
Inspector Twelfth Lighthouse District.

San Juan Arrives.
The Pacific Mall Steamship San Juan arrived
yesterday from Panama and way ports. She
brought S58 tons of cargo, sixty-three packages
of treasure valued at $7S,905 SO, and passen
gers as follows :

The wreck of the schooner Eureka was sold
yesterday by auction at the Merchants' Ex
change. She was bought by Captain Harry
Goodall, who paid $105 for her.
Fire was discovered yesterday morning In
the roof of the saloon at 133 East street Tbe
building, which is owned by Harvey Downey,
was damaged to the extent of $400 before the
flames were extinguished. . Â¦

Fire in Roof.
W. Swan, an apprentice on the British ship
Kynance, fell down the vessel's hold yester
day and sustained some severe abrasions which
were treated at the Harbor Hospital.

Zealandia Departs.
THE Oceanic Steamship Company's
liner Sonoma, Captain von Otcr
endorp. arrived yesterday morn
ing from Australia with 262 pas
sengrers. a large cargo and th<?
Australian-British mails. In spite of
rough weather between Auckland and
Pcgo Pago and a heavy fog encountered
off this port the Sonoma reached port
ahead of schedule time.
Among the Sonorr.a's passengers was
Commander Blocklinger of the navy and
lately in command oX the gunboat Wheel
ing at Pago Pago. He was accompanied
fajr Mrs. Bloeklinger and is home on sick
leave.
The Sonoma brought from Honolulu the
three mates and thirteen sailors of the
ill-fated British ship Fannie ICerr, which
was destroyed by fire last month about
7iO miles to the northward of the Ha
waiian Islands. Captain Gibbons and his
wife remained in Honolulu.
A. L. Dearlove, who has been in Ho
nolulu in connection with the new Pa
cific cable, was a passenger on the So
noma, and E. J. Russell, the representa
tive in Australia of the Standard Oil
Company, was also on board.
On the Sonoma's outward voyage L.
Leonard Ferguson, an oiler, was killed
by being caught in the shaft. The acci
dent occurred soon after leaving Pago
Pago. Deceased was burled at Auckland.
The Sonoma left Sydney on June 3 and
â¢was j' days 12 hours coming up from Ho
nolulu. The drought situation in Aus
tralia was still serious when the Sonoma
?a.iied. but the plague appeared to have
been checked.
There were no deaths from the latter
cause the week before the liner left.
There were three stowaways on the So
noma: one from Auckland, the others
from Honolulu.
Among the passengers were:

This, however, may become compli
cated by propositions to attach conditions
to the concurrence, one of the conditions
under discussion being a time limit for
the period of negotiation to secure the
perfected title. ' y_
The friends of the Panama project have
been making a canvass of the strength
of the Senate amendment in the House
and are confident that a motion to concur
can command a majority.
The House conferees appeared at the
appointed time, 2. o'clock., but through a
misunderstanding the Senate conferees
were not on hand, and the meeting went
over until 11 b' clock to-morrow.
The length of the contest will depend,
therefore, on the, duration of the insist
ence of Hepburn and Davey for the Nica
ragua route and the attitude of -the
House in supporting such an insistence.
There Is little indication as to how long
this insistence will last, but statements
made by the House conferees lead to the
belief that the Insistence will not. be car
ried, to the point of a permanent dead
lock, defeating all 'canal legislation at
this session.
WASHINGTON, June 23.â The contem
plated conference on the isthmian canal
bill led to considerable comment to-dajr.
on the outcome of this important meas
ure. There is no doubt that the House
conferees will at the outset make strenu
ous insistence on the Nicaragua route, as
provided by the House bill.
Hepburn and Davey are earnestly In
favor of the Nicaragua route, and they
undoubtedly will stand by the House bill
as long as there is any possibility of its
selection. On the other hand, Fletcher of
Minnesota considers the Panama route
better than the Nicaragua, although he
thinks fuller investigation has shown the
Darien route better than either of the
others. . However, as the present legisla
tive issue appears to be confined to a
choice between Nicaragua and Panama,
Fletcher probably will cast his influence
for the latter. â¢ â¢ .. '

NOTE â In the above exposition of the tides
the early morning tides are given in -the lef i
hand column and the successive tides of the
day in the order of occurrence as to time: the
fourth time column g-lves the last tide of tbs
day, except when there are but three tides as
sometimes occurs. The heights given are" in
addition to the soundings of the United States
Coast Survey charts, except when a minus ( â )
sign precedes the height, and then the number
given i is subtracted frcm the depth given by
the charts. The plane of reference is the mean
of the lower low waters.

assan, the offlcA-s and representatives of

Phillips Glazier is the supreme chief. Mrs.
Glazier is a* resident of Kort Madison,
Iowa, and is said to be an unusually able
executive' officer, remarkably well versed
in oarliamentary law. The ladies of 'this
branch of Pythianisnvhave already out
lined a plan to be carried out during their
stay in this city. Committees will en
tertain the ladies wh/> shall accompany
the Knights, and thes'e have arranged for
receptions, parties, balls, excursions, and
the distribution of flowers and fruit to be
furnished them from a number of the
counties?" -

mate there will be 100,000 strangers in San

RATHBONE SISTERS WILL
VISIT WITH PYTHIANS
OWING-io the high price of beef and the'gen
eral clamor against the meat trust more at
tention has been given than would other
wise have been accorded to a report, of W. H. Wiley,
chief of the Bureau of Chemistry of the Department
of Agriculture, on the relative values \)f various kinds
of foods. There is nothing particularly novel in his
statements. The world has been" told over and over
that there are lots of foods better than beef as well
as cheaper. In times past, however, the average
American has given little heed to such statements.
Things are different now. Chief Wiley does not
speak more eloquently nor more authoritatively than
some other folks on the subject, but the ears of the
people are more attentive, because this time the
pocket as well as' the stomach is interested.
We have-not seen a complete copy of the report
of the chief, but a summary is given out, for the press
quotes him as saying that only. 25 per cent or the
total weight of meat purchased is of any nutritive
value; that even if it were all nutritive and a man
got his money's worth out of all meat he pays for,:
its use would still be inexpedient, at breakfast, at any
rate, and might be profitably dispensed at lunch.
The chief is quoted as saying: "In so far as actual
nourishment is concerned the very cheapest and best
that can be secured is presented by the cereals, viz.,
Indian corn, wheat, oats, rye, rice, etc. These bodies
contain all the nourishment necessary to supply the -
waste of the body arid the energy and heat necessary
to all animal functions and hard labor, in a form well
fluted to digestion, and capable' riot only of maintain
ing the body : in a perfect condition, but also; of fur
nishing the energy necessary to the hardest, kind of
manual labor. .The; waste material in cereals is very
small, and, as compared with that in meats, practi
cally none at all. In fact, the ordinary wastes, such
as the bran and germ, are among the most nutritive
components of the cereals, and both! health and
economy would be conserved as a rule by their con
sumption, instead of rejecting them, a ; s in the ordin
ary processes of milling.'-'- ,;Â¦ ' r r
The world, has v heard that wisdom before, but this
time it is doubly valuable. It affords a means of,
bringing the proud beef barons to terms. Let the
Eastern people as one man take to cornstarch and
vegetables. Things will then be them
and very unhealthy for the trust. The experiment is
worth trying,- and now that i the East is , so' earnestly
discussing the cereal philosophy it* is to be hoped
her people will live up to it/ r >.- '/; â¢â¢'*','
ACCORDING to Santos Dumont the problem
of aerial voyaging is much nearer solution
than most people imagine. He"says men talk
about aerial navigation now as they talked of auto
mobile locomotion ten- years ago, "believing they
were predicting the future without suspecting that
they, spoke of the present."- .He asserts that the
..practical use of airships will not be delayed until the
coming of some future generation, but will be at
ained within a time comparatively short.
As to the merits of airships' formed of balloons as
compared wifh flying machines or aeroplanes he de
clares himself in favor of the former, but points out
that his own airship i^ a combination of the two. It
has a balloon structure, but is nevertheless heavier
than air. It rises only because of the force supplied
by the propeller, and when the propeller ceases the
ship descends to the earth by its own weight.
In the current number of the North American Re
view he gives an elaborate presentation of the sub
ject and argues that with a little further improve-!
ment in the way of diminished weight and increased'
power of propulsion the. flying-ship will be accom
plished.
He says: "I am persuaded that the airship, thanks
to the hydrogen (carried in the balloon), will always
have an advantage over the aeroplane in being able
to. carry a great quantity of combustible material for
long journeys, a number of travelers and a consid
erable weight of merchandiseâ an advantage that
will give it a practical utility unquestionably' superior
in commerce and in war. On the other hand, the
flying machine will without doubt attain for short
distances an incomparable/ speed, the advantages of
which will be in certain cases very appreciable.
* * *. But when I think that an airship of the
length of the trans- Atlantic 'steamer Deutschland,
constructed with the proportions of my No. 6, would
transport a thousand voyagers of my own weight
with a sufficiently powerful motor and the necessary
amount of petroleum, from New York to Havre in
two days, I cannot help finding the dirigible balloon
more interesting than .the aeroplane from an
economic point' of view, which is the dominant one
in the world to-day."*
â¢ With the spirit of a true enthusiast Santos Dumont
declares that aerial voyaging will " soon . be " accom
plishecfTf only mankind would take more interest in
it. He says he knows of a dozen petroleum balloons
owned by men rich enough to make aerial experi
ments, and yet they will not enter competitions and
thus help to excite public, interest. He announces
an intention to appeal to the world . through maga
zines and books, and says: "I shall hot be content
till every person is an aeronaut, either in airships or
in- aeroplanes." It is a big undertaking, but not
necessarily a vain one. Meantime the advocates of
the aeroplane are working as diligently to perfect a
flying machine as Santos .Dumont is / to perfect : an
airship. He -has therefore enough competition to
keep his enthusiasm at fever â¢ heat, and the' world
waits expectantly ready to applaud the winncr-

Oakland Enquirer.
Criticizes Action of Gage.
Where was Governor Gage libeled and
where should he be most anxious to se
cure a vindication? The Call, which was
the ( head libeler, is published and largely
circulated In San Francisco. It is not be
lieved that it has a large circulation in
San Pedro, Los Angeles County, for none
of the San Francisco papers have. The
Chronicle, the chief assistant libeler, is
also a San Francisco paper, and the
Times is published in Los Angeles. ]t
might be logical to prosecute the Times
in San Pedro, but we can see neither
logic nor wit in the decision of the Gov
ernor to seek his vindication from San
Francisco ,slander in the little seaside
town on the southern coast. As we un
derstand it, the Governor's object in
prosecuting is to prove to all the people
that he has been falsely vilified, and
we should suppose he would seek to do
this in the largest forum in the State,
not in a hole or a corner, other words!
fean Francisco, where the offense was
committed, and where there . are more
people than in any other town to wit
ness the refutation, is the place of all
places where the refuting should begone

AIRSHIP DEVELOPMENT.
REPORTS from Rome concerning the negoti
ations with the Vatican for the purpose of
purchasing the lands now held by the friars
in the Philippines are encouraging, but vague. It is
announced that the Cardinals appointed by the Pope
to I act for the church are in agreement with
Governor Taft on all' essential pbints, but that
there arc wide differences on matters of detail.
Neither side has given out any intimation of the na
ture of the issues on which the differences occur,
but it is probable the question of the amount to be
paid for the lands constitutes one of the subjects of
disagreement, as a report from -Washington some
time ago announced that our .Government estimates
the value of the lands at about $7,000,000, while the
church estimate is not less than $10,000,000. . ,
Another cause of difficulty in reaching an agree
ment is probably to be found in the character of the
credentials given to. Governor Taft. He has not
gone to Rome as an accredited Minister from this
country, but simply as an agent to negotiate a busi
ness matter. In the instructions given to the Gov
ernor it is expressly stated: "Your errand will not
be in any sense diplomatic in its nature, but will
be purely a business matter of negotiation by you as
Governor of the Philippines for the purchase of
property from the owners thereof, and the settlement
of land titles in such a manner as to contribute to
the best interests of the people of the islands." â¢
Even in the restricted sense of a business agent the
Governor has not full liberty of action, for whatever
he does must be subject to action by Congress. Thus
the Governor is instructed "to take up the subject
tentatively with the ecclesiastical superiors, %ho
must ultimately determine the friars' course of con
duct, and endeavor to reach at least a basis of nego
tiation along lines which will be satisfactory to them
and to the Philippine government, accompanied by
a full' understanding on both sides of the facts and
the views and purposes of the parties to the negotia
tion, so that when Congress shall have_ acted the
business may proceed to conclusion without delay."
The measure is one of the highest importance to
the people of the Philippines and to ourselves. In a
recent statement as to the provisions of the Philip
pine bill on the subject Senator Lodge said: "It
provides adequate and comprehensive land laws,
which have been wofully lacking during and since
the time Spain lost supremacy there. Under the
laws of Spain there was no such thing as a title to
land. The occupants of the land exercised no owner
ship of it, and as a result they were constantly in a
state of turmoil. Of the 72,000,000 acres of land
there only 5,000,000 acres were held by private indi
viduals. The remaining 68,000,000 acres were held by
Spain. Under this bill homesteads of forty acres
each are to be given to the natives, and .they will
have a clear title to the land. The 450,000 acres of
land which the friars hold, which is the best agricul
tural land on the islands, is to be purchased, and this
is to be disposed of to those who now occupy these
lands. This must necessarily bring about better con
ditions in the way of wages to the Filipinos and in
other .important respects."
There is little doubt that Congress will agree to
terms that may be found satisfactory to the church
and to the Philippine government. Hardly any
thing we could do will more potently conduce to the
welfare of the islands than settling land titles and
giving the industrious and thrifty among tne people
a chance to establish homes of their own upon lands
that are theirs in fee simple. It is also in the interest
of the friars to get rid of the lands, which they can
not profitably use -under present conditions. Such
being the case, it can be readily believed that the two
parties arc already agreed upon the essential points of
the negotiation. The land will be sold, theÂ» United
States will guarantee the payment, and the old strife
between the Filipinos 'and the friars will end
amicably and in a manner beneficial to all concerned.'
PHILIPPINE LAND VALUES.
Professor J. C. Merriam of the depart
ment of geolotry said that he did not care
to go on record as stating positively that
the phenomenon was due ,to Pelee's
ashes, but asserted that suchVwas very
possible.
"In such violent eruptions as the recent
one in Martinique," said he, "the vol
canic dust or scoria rises to an enormous
height, so high, in fact, as to be above
the air currents of the lower air strata.
This ash; being of very light weight, is
not influenced much by gravity, and re
mains suspended high in the air for long
periods of time."
Professor Merriam cited the instance
of the eruption of Mount Krakatoa, in
Sumatra, when the volcanic ash was car
ried completely around the earth and re
mained suspended in the air for more
than a year.
"Only two things could bring about this
appearance of a circle about- the sun
either the presence of; mpistwre in the
higher strata of air or the presence of
some very finq material, such as volcanic
dust or ash. During the present dry con
dition of the atmosphere it" is scarcely
credible that there can.be much moisture
in the air, hence it is doubtless the ashes
which are producing the ring about the
sun. The same cause also would bring
about a very fiery red sunset. We have
been expecting such sunsets for some
time. The trade winds, which have been
blowing steadily from east to west during
the last month, together with the revolu
tion of the earth, would bring <is within
the zone of the volcanic ash."
â¢Dr. Tracy Crawford, fellow in astron
omy and assistant at the students' ob
servatory, had the following explanation
to give concerning the phenomenon:
BERKELEY, June 23.â Considerable
comment was caused to-day by the phe
nomenon of a large circle appearing
around the sun when it had reached its
meridian at noontime to-day, and much
speculation has-been indulged in as to
the cause of the occurrence. The opinion
of university professors, who are calcu
iated to know about such appearances,
is that the spectacle is due to the pres
ence in the air of fine volcanic dust from
the volcano of Mont Pelee.

Forecaster McAdle of the Weather Bu
reau declines to think that the phenome
non was a celestial warning of impe'nd
ing calamity. He says that It is very un
usual to . see so perfect a halo, but that
there are records of many less striking
spectacles of a similar character. The
halo had a radius of 22 degrees. It is
said to have been caused by the refrac
tion of light by ice 'crystals in the vale
of cirro-stratus clouds â a combination of
the light fleecy and the layer-like clouds.
Scientists attributed the extraordinary
heat* in the Eastern. States a few years
ago to disturbances on the sun's surface.
A similar appearance to that witnessed
here yesterday and one which was ob
servable all .over the world in the summer
of 1889 was said to be due to the fact that
the earth was passing through a haze oc
casioned by-Infinitesimal particles of mat
ter left in space as a result of the de
struction of the asteroids.
Science and history both furnish the
warrant for the idea that solar influences
occasion terrestrial disturbances. Lytto:i,
in the "Last Days of Pompeii," tells of
the peculiar appearance of the sun before
the -eruption of Vesuvius, as do also the
letters of Pliny. It is related that the sun
appeared dull and heavy* and was sur
rounded with 'a. ring just before the Mar
tinique disaster. It is a legend in Sicily
that the sun caused terror to people in
the vicinity a few days prior to the time
when Stromboli of the Lipari Islands
reared its head above the Mediterranean.
The followers of Zoroaster direct the
courses of their lives by the appearance
of the sun .and moon. Throughout all
history it has been - customary to take
warning from solar phenomena.
Solar Â¦ halos are not very unusual. So
perfect a one as that observable here yes
terday is, however, remarkable. The phe
nomenon is frequently seen in the tropics
and in the southern hemisphere, but or
dinarily rather indistinctly. Such appear
ances are. considered- by- savage races and
ithe superstitious portion of civilized peo
-ples as portents of : volcanic eruptions,
earthquakes, terrible storms, or some
other great disaster.
"What does it mean?" "Is it a sign' of
a cyclone or an earthquake?" "I never
saw anything like that before." Such
and other similar expressions were onjthe
lips of every one when the great solar"
halo appeared in the skies early yesterday
afternoon.: In appearance it was an im
mense ' ring surrounding the sun and
seemed to have a circumference about fif
teen times greater than that of the source
of light. No two persons could agree on
its color. Some said brown, others black,
and still others declared it was exactly
like a rainbow. The phenomenon was
first observed at about noon. Its effect
was mo3t pronounced at a quarter after
one o'clock. During the afternoon it fad
ed gradually and .was. barely perceptible
toward sundown. ' . .
SOCIETY FOLK
GOING INTO
THE COUNTRY
Coil says he sleeps a great deal. He
attributes his longevity and excellent
health to the fact that he has always
been regular in his habits.
Coil's grandfather died at the age of
104 years. The old gentleman says he
has chewed tobacco ever since he was 10
years old, but rarely smoked. He, has
never been sick a day in his life; fe en
joying good health at present; his eye
sight is almost perfect and his hearing
is excellent; he is able to get around
well; he never had the toothache, and
would to-day have-a full set of teeth but
for the fact that the withering of the
gums allowed several of them to drop
out.
OLUSA, June 23.-James Coil, who
/f^ resides in the famous quicksilver
(I .mining town of Sulphur Creek
'^^ will celebrate his one hundredth
, birthday on the 16th of next Janu
ary. Coil was born in Warren County,
Tennessee, in 1S03. His father was of
Irish and his mother of German descent
Coil - came to California in 1313 by ox
team across the plains, and the encoun
ters with Indians and narrow escapes or
his party would make an interestln.,
story. He returned to his family at *ay
etteville. Ark., in 1S52 and remained there
until 1S5S, when he again came to- tha
Golden State," determined to make a stake
and prepare a home for his family. Luck
was against him, and since that day of
parting in 1S5S he has never seen any
member of his family, nor has he heard
from any of them since the war closed m
1S65. i'X'tfi ;^^
By occupation Coil was a trader in
livestock in the 'East. He owned many
stages in the early days and at- one time
conducted a large hotel in Jefferson City.
Mo. When he came to California he en
gaged in mining with more or Jess suc
cess. The old gentleman has traveled
over the greater, part of the United
States and has seen and conversed with
several American ' Presidents, among
them Jefferson and Jackson.

S~~\ NGE more the proprietor and the 'manager of The Call- have. ; been arrested
f 'Â¦Â¦:.'. V/upon:a charge of publishing a' criminal libel against Governor Gage in conneo
V : ,f tibn with the ; exposure of frauds committed Jn 'the administration of the San
S Quentin prison. -This time the complaint is sworn out in this city and the de
fendants are held for trial 'here. They are ready. It remains to be seen whether the Gov- \
-Surely it is high time to bring this case to a hearing; Two arrests with no trial \
would be something in the nature of a farce. If Gage really believe he can benefit his
reputation or improve his political chances by having a judicial- inquiry into the truth 01
falseness. of the statements published by The Gall in connection with the violations of
law at . San ' Quentin, he has now an opportunity to obtain tjiat benefit without delay: â¢
He can give to the public no satisfactory reason for shirking the issue raised by
the latest arrest of the defendants, nor for seeking to evade a trial in San Francisco. He
may select any court he chooses to hear the^case. The defendants will interpose no ob
jection. They ask for nothing but a prompt trial and a fair and full hearing of all J:he
evidence. . ' ~" J
The second arrest is annoying, but is not without compensating advantages. It
brings the suit at a place where there will be no difficulty in proceeding at once to trial.
The defendants are here, the witnesses are here and the prison records' and documents
can be readily brought here. There is but one doubtful; phase in the case and that is
involved in the question whether Gage will come to the front and make. a straight fight,
or whether he will anew shift, shirk, dodge and try to fly' to a court where a speedy
trial is not obtainable.
The. sentimental considerations that led the Governor to seek at San Pedro a bath
for his reputation, which he alleges to have been soiled, should now give way to consider
ations of another kind. He asserts that he has been libeled, maligned and' injured by the
defendants; he declares he cannot be satisfied by a civil suit, but desires to have them
punished as criminals; he avows that he wishes the trial to be held promptly so that he
can get a vindication in court in time to submit it to the Republican State Convention,
before which he is to go as a candidate, for a renomination.. Upon such assertions,
declarations and avowals the defendants offer him the opportunity he says he so much
desires. They are ready to go forward with the trial at once. v .
Governor Gage must bear in mind that his is not the only reputation that is at
stake in this issue. The charges made in his complaint against the proprietor and the
manager of The Call are false and malicious. Had they emanated from any other than a
discredited man, or been published in any other form than a court document/ they
would constitute a valid basis for criminal libel against himself. Having made such
charges he is called upon to meet the issue. If he now shirk a trial he will not
only exhibit a consciousness of the falseness of his charges, but reveal in his make-up a
There is another phasef of the issue that is not to be overlooked. So far as
the statements concerning the frauds at San Quentin affect 'Gage at all, they -affect him
in his official capacity as Governor of California. Even\upon his own allegations, it is not
his private but his official reputation that is at stake. It is therefore a strange proceeding on
his" part to run away from his official residence at Sacramento, where the prison re
ports are on file, and endeavor to get the case heard in a remote town, where it is
doubtful if all the witnesses and documents in the case could be brought until after
months of waiting. : -
Finally, it is to be borne in mind that the issue transcends private interests and
affects the politics and the property, of the State. - The frauds at San Quentin, whose
exposure the Governor -asserts has affected his reputation, are matters that concern all
the people of California. The public, therefore, has a right to demand that a full inves
tigation be made and a full hearing be had before a judicial tribunal. Moreover,, the voters
have a right to demand that the inquiry be made before the'approaching gubernatorial
campaign opens.
The defendants in the case recognize the right of the public^to ; a full knowledge
of all the facts. They are eager for an investigation. They have sought to obtain one
from the start. They urged the State Board of Prison Directors to make, one; and in
'the hope that such would be the case they suspended for a time further publication in
The Call of the facts in the case. It was not until the board showed signs of evading an
inquiry, and the Governor was detected removing public documents and making an in
quiry of his own, secretly, that The Call resumed the task of exposing the frauds. Now
again the defendants come forward asking and demanding a trial.
We repeat then: the issue has\ been made up, the defendants are ready. Is

Park and Outdoor Association
San Francisco Branch of American
Advances RapidlyX
Reports made yesterday at the weekly
meeting of the San Francisco branch of
the American Park and Outdoor Associa
tion, held in the rooms of the California
Club, show that much work has been done
of late. The members of the society, who
are workins for the beautincatlon of the
schools and other public buildings of San
Francisco, have chosen three schoolhouses
âthe Lafayette, Rincon Grammar and
Mission Highâ as objects of adornment.
Photographs of these three structures
taken by Professor Michel of the Girla'
High School and plans drawn by. P. S.
Barbour have been sent to W. Manning
and O. C. Simmons of Chicago, who will
make the final designs, which. will be
sent here, to be followed out by the so
society.
Tho society has also formed a commit
tee to locate the most desolate part of
the city, for the purpose of beautifying
and improving the same. The work being
cone is meeting with the approval of the
Mayor, Park Commissioners and 'School
Directors.
Assessor Dodge informed the Board of
Supervisors yesterday that this year's as
sessment roll would aggregate at least
$420,000,000. This will make sure certain
appropriations made for sewer and street
work on the assumption that the roll
would be in excess of $405,000,000. Dodge
filed the following statement:
Gentlemen of the Board of Supervisors: I
appreciate fully the difficulty which your board
has had to contend with, that is: To provide
for a more expensive government under the
charter with a restricted rate of taxation,
namely, the dollar rate. I am aware, If some
others are not, that the appropriations made by
your Honorable Board for running expenses are
practically no greater this year than they have
been for the last two years, and I am aware
ot the fact that you have less available for
public improvements this year, because . you
have no surplus revenue from an increased as
sessment roll to expend for these improvements,
as has* been the case for the last two years.
Knowing these facts, I have endeavored by
every means in my power to assist you In your
financial troubles. â¢ Â¦ - Â¦ '.
It is with no small degree of pleasure that I
find that 1 can now assure you of a roll of at
least $420,000,000. This is $7,000,000 in excess
ot last year's roll, and will place at your dis
posal at a conservative estimate at least $80,000
more than you have counted on having. I am
enabled to give you this estimate at this early
date, two weeks ahead of the time that it has
ever been possible to make it before, for the
reason that I was allowed by your Honorable
Board this year $t$000 for extra help. Regard
ing this help, I can further say that without it
I would have had no such surplus. This brings
me to the consideration of the same extra ap
propriation for the coming- fiscal year. I I now
ask you, gentlemen, to restore to the budget
this appropriation of $8000, which you made,
and which his Honor has vetoed, with the ex
planation that the money could be provided
later on from the urgent necessity fund if
needed. I know that it is needed, and I know
further that the money may not be in the ur
gent necessity fund later on, and further that'
this fund is not intended to meet such ex
penditures. With this appropriation assured
to me I can promise to give this board the total
of the roll next' year, when you first consider
the budget, several weeks earlier than this date.
You will then have tiie advantage of knowing
what surplus revenue yiu will have available.
This will overcome the embarrassing fact that
you must otherwise fix your appropriation be
fore you know what you have to spend.
Dodge explained that there would be a
possible delinquency of $8,000,000, including'
that of three big corporations, .which
would make an actual collection of taxes
on $412,000,000, giving $70,000 additional for
improvements.
cause they were deemed too -high, and the
clerk was directed to readvertise lor pro
posals. Supervisor Payot wanted the
resolution on the subject sent back to
the Printing Committee, but Supervisor
Wynn objected. .
"The committee has already carefully
considered the bids," said wynn, "and"
we found the prices too high. It did not
desire to present these contractors with a
silver crowbar with which to break into
the city treasury, and we therefore rec
ommended that their bids be rejected." â¢

Damage Case Submitted.
The damage suit of Dolbeer and others,
owners of the steam schooner Iaqua,
against the Austrian steamer Maria was
submitted on briefs in the United State3
District Court yesterday. Th^ action was
brought to recovei $50,000 salvage and $440
for damages alleged to .have been sus
tained in a collision. '
Charles E. Vannatta,. superintendent,
regular force and office/boy of the Van
natta Detective Agency, the office of
waich is at 4024 Twenty-third street, is
being sued for divorce by his wife. Van
natta, in his capacity of private detec
tive, had ferreted out evidence that has
assisted many an Ill-mated pair In gain
ing a separation, but he will scarcely
need to search for evidence in this case
In her complaint Mrs. Vannatta
charges her husband with having treated
her with extreme cruelty and with having
threatened her life, besides using vulgar
language in addressing her on several
occasions. But these are not her more
serious accusations. She accuses him of
having deserted her for another wema
and states that that woman is Miss Edith
Plerson, her own sister. She avers that
her husband and Miss Plerson cohabited
in a house on Ellis street, and in several
other localities.
When seen last night Mrs. Vannatta
told a pitiable tale of ill-treatment and
neglect. She said that Vannatta and her
self were wedded in Chicago in 1896 and
that at thatitime he was assistant super
rntendent of the Pinkerton Detective
Agency. For a year they lived happily
together and she invited her sister to
come and share their felicity. It was then
she claims that her troubles commenced.
Her husband became enamored with the
sister's charms and lavished all his affec
tions on her. - :
When Vannatta received the appoint
ment to the office of superintendent of
the Pinkerton Agency , in this city Miss
Edith Plerson was Invited to accompany
him and bis wife here. OC her experience
'.n this city Mrs. Vannatta has the fol
lowing to say:
The past two years has been like a long con
tinued nightmare to me. Both my sister, who
is five years younger than I am, and my hus
band, have treated me most cruelly. < He has
lived with her right in my own house and has
remained locked in a room with her for houw
at a time. I was left at home to do all the
work while he took her to French dinners and
to theaters. For two years I have worn the
same dress, while he dressed her in the height
of style. I was scarcely ever seen with him
on the street, but she was always with him.
Daring this month he told one that he was
going to Santa Cruz on a business trip but I
subsequently learned that he had arranged to
meet my sister there. He hired a room for
her in a hotel and' frequently visited her I
have begged my sister not to come between me
and my husband, but she would not listen to
me. When she went with him to Santa Cruz
she gave up a good position which she held
out in the Western Addition. I promised my
sister that if she would let me have my hus
band I would forgive her. It was only after
I had decided | to commence suit that I wrote
to our mother, telling her what kind of a girl
my sister had turned out to be. My sister has
broken up my home. ,
Vannatta has not been connected with
the Pinkertons since about two years ago
when the agency demanded his resigna
tion. Since that time he has been'con
ducting a private agency aTnd had his of
lice In his residence. .
Wife of Detective Van
natta Is Seeking
Divorce. \
THE Board of Supervisors by a
unanimous vote, as required by
the charter, finally passed the
two ordinances levying a special
tax of IVz cents on every $100 of
assessed valuation for new schoolhouses
and 7% cents for a new City and County
Hospital yesterday. Before action was
taken It was necessary to take a recess of
nearly one hour and a half to give Super
visor Sanderson, who had left his sickbed
to cast his vote, an opportunity to reach
the assembly room.
While waiting for Sanderson several
city officials were heard In regard to the
vetoes of tht yarious items in the budget
When Sanderson arrived he was carried
in a chair from his carriage to his desk,
and without any discussion the first ordi
nance, suspending the dollar limit of . the
charter to the extent of 7% cents to build
a new hospital, was read by title and put
to a vote by the Mayor. The roll was
called at once in order to detain Sander
son as little as possible, as it was plain to
see that he was a very sick man. One
after another the Supervisors answered
"Aye," and Sanderson's rolled out as
strong as the rest of them.
A DRAMATIC SCENE.
The scene was dramatic in the extreirf-e
and all eyes were centered on Sanderson,
who was braving the ordeal In good style,
surrounded by his attendants, who
watched his every move. It was stated
that Sanderson had experienced another
paralytic stroke while on his way to the
City Hall and a recurrence of it was
feared. When he was brought out of it he
was urged to go back home, but he reso
lutely ordered the carriage to drive on so
that he could record his vote in favor of
what he had deemed improvements of ex
treme urgency as contemplated by the
charter. \
No sooner had the Mayor announced
that the ordinance levying an extra tax of
7% cents for a new hospital had been
finally passed than the second ordinance,
imposing air additional' tax of 7^ cents to
purchase sites and to erect new school
houses, was put to a vote. Again the ayes
were told off by each Supervisor, and San
derson, who, during the preliminary skir
mish on both ordinances, had interposed
a mild opposition, voted with his fellows
and new schoolhouses were made a cer
tainty by the final passage of the second
ordinance.
After the result had been announced
Mayor Schmltz advanced to Sanderson
and chatted for a moment with him. In
the meantime Braunhart had taken the
chair and put the Mayor's veto of the va
rious items In the budget to a vote.

CLAIMS SISTER
WRECKED HOME
Supervisors Finally Pass the Two Ordinances Suspending Dollar
Limit of Taxation â Sanderson Leaves His Sick Bed in Order
to Make the Vote Unanimous as Required by. the Charter Terms
ADOPT SPECIAL LEVY FOR NEW
HOSPITAL AND SCH00LH0USES
The Board of Supervisors adopted a
resolution yesterday awarding the con
tract for lighting the public streets to the
San Francisco Gas and Electric Company.
The contract secures street lighting: for
321) nights, with the conditions that the
Board of Public Works Bhall designate
during the year the nights when illumina
tion is not required, not to exceed thirty
six In the year. This will insure practi
cally every-night lighting during the fog
gy and winter season, and an extension
of the system to the number of 100 new
gas lights and 50 arc lights. The rates
under this contract will be: For single
gas lamp per night, .087 cents; triple-top
gas lamps per night, 15 cents; arc lights
,per night, 37 cents.
These rates depend upon the increase
of twenty-nine nights lighted over the
old schedule, and will require the original
appropriation of $275,000. The contracts
for lighting the public buildings are not
fully prepared, there being some difficulty
in adjusting the rates upon certain- build
ings in the outside dictricts so as to com
ply with the charter provision restricting
the rates to the minimum charge of other
consumers.
The Mayor appointed Braunhart a
member of the Committee on Charter
Amendments, in place of Brandenstein,
resigned.
The petition of R. J. Curtln, W. I. Rear
dan and G. Varcoe, deputies in the Tax
Collector's office, that their salaries be
paid for a period, of time they were pre
vented from performing their duties, was
denied. â¢
ST. MARYS PARK FUND.
The ordinance authorizing the payment
of $25,000 out of St. Mary's Park fund to
J. \V. Goldstein and others to satisfy
judgments obtained in the condemnation
of their lands for park purposes was de
feated on final passage. Braunhart gave
notice of reconsideration at the next
meeting.
Ordinances were passed to print provid
ing for amendments to the charter pro
vision regarding the method to be fol
lowed in the acquisition of public utilities
or bonded improvements, so that all such
utilities or improvements may be submit
ted to the people at one election. One
amendment provides that in securing es
timates of the cost of original construc
tion of water works the Supervisors must
procure plans and estimates of the cost*
of obtaining from such sources as they
may designate as available a sufficient
supply of pure water for the city. Plans
and estimates of public utilities, these
, ordinances provide, shall be procured
only when the Supervisors determine to
acquire the same or when the electors
petition for the acquisition thereof, in
stead of overy two years, as now required
by the charter.
The San Francisco Retail Merchants'
Association submitted the draft of an or
dinance fixing the license to be paid by
drugstores where spirituous liquors are
sold at 52 per annum. It was referred to
j the License Committee.
CHARITY NOT TO BENEFIT.
The resolution requiring the payment of
55O00 to some charity by the club bring
ing: off the Jeffries-Fitzslmmons contest
\cas indefinitely postponed.
Connor objected to having money given
to charity through a boxing contest. He
contended that the club was giving up
enough as it was.
"Suppose one of these men should kill
the other, no charitable organization
would take the money," said Connor.
"There is no telling but we will have to
stop all these boxing contests at some
time, especially if any murder should re
sult."
Brandenstein held that the proper way
would be to increase the tax for boxing
contests to $5000 to help run the city gov
ernment.
The City Attorney was requested to
give his opinion as to whether the city

CHICAGO. Jure - 23. â Grain bears were !
caught in a traa and were squeezed to the j
good profit of the bulls. Clearing weather In.
the wheat fields, as shown by the weather map
at the opening, augmented by lower cables, In
duced liberal early liquidation and short selling
of wheat. After last week's bullish arguments
on 'wet weather, followed by Saturday's break,
the bears thought the prices were much too
high for the fair, warm weather they had In
their mind for the harvests. The excitement
over wheat scarcity abroad seemed to have '
subsided and this gave the bears encourage
ment. Bulls who had paper profits that were <
threatened hastened to take them. As a result
July opened %@?ic to y 3 @%c down at 72%Â©>
73c. There were a few early bullish statistics
that were lost Bight of for a time, but soon the
crowd perceived that the pit was oversold.
Big increases, in stocks frightened the shorts
and the rest of the day the trade v/as busy
pushing prices up as the shorts cried for cover.
July worked up rapidly to 74V4@74%c with ap
parent ease. Buying was general. There was
a little dip from top prices on profit-taking,
but July closed strong, %c up at 74yi<g>74%c.
Corn was very nervous all day. Early in
the day there was marked artificial strength In
July options, caused by arbitrary high bids by
the bull leaders without considerable pur
chases. Fluctuations were /erratic. Too much
rain and cold weather has ben a bullish
factor in corn of late aiid in some sections of
the corn belt, especially in Iowa, these condl- '
tlons now prevail to some extent. In other
sections, however, crops are said to be in good
condition. July closed l%c up at C7c
Oats were strengthened by the advances Jn i
the other grains and by their own cash situa
tion. July closed firm, i,4c up, at 40%c.
Provisions again made top prices for the
year. Hogs sold at $7 85 at the yards, also the
year's record. Packers now hold almost every
thing in the pork list and yield up vnry little
of it. This condition naturally brought sharp
advances. July pork closed 32*Ac up. lard" 15c
up and ribs 12"4c higher. The cash demand
held up fairly well, but there seemed still to
be a strong manipulative aspect to the pit.
The leading futures ranged as follows:".

Deciduous and Citrus Fruits.
Receipts of : Longworth Strawberries were

receipts of crates from Loomls. - A few chests

most of the offerings commanded full figures.
Raspberries and Gooseberries met with prompt
sale.
Cherries were easier and the bulk of loose
stock went to the canners. The receipts from
the river consisted chiefly of poor stock which
sold at the bottom quotations.
There was a steady Inquiry for choice de
ciduous fruits, but the receipts of green stock
were very large and the market continued
dull. Only good ripe stock sold well.

Potatoes,. Onions and Vegetables.
Receipts of Potatoes were light yesterday,
but owing to 'the heavy accumulation of poor
stock during the past week the market con
tinued dull except for fancy stock, which was
in limited supply and commanded the usual
premium over the quotations.
Most of the receipts of red Onions were con
signed to one house and were firmly held at
the top quotation.
Offerings of Tomatoes and Cucumbers were
large and prices continued to weaken. Peas
and String Beans met with ready sale at sus
tained prices, some fancy String from Alameda
County selling at 3\i.c per lb. There was very
little Inquiry for Rhubarb or Asparagus and
offerings dragged. Summer Squash In large
boxes was lower. A 10-lb box of Bell Pep
pers was received from Sacramento and was
offering at 40c per lb but was not sold.

WEATHER CONDITIONS AND GENERAL
FORECAST.
The pressure has risen slowly over Caliornia.
A well-marked depress'en overlies Eastern
Warhintrton, Eastern Oregon and Nevada. This
may cause unsettled weather and rain gener
ally over the northern half of the Pacific slope.
The temperatures have iallen from 6 to 20
desiees over Washington and Oregon, ana '
f-ooU-r weather may be expected Tuesday night
In Ccotral and Northern California. Along the
ecaEt from San Francisco to -Kan Diego there
has been a rapid rise, and 'from Point Con
ception to Point Dume the temperatures are
fu:iy IS degrees wanner than usual. A maxi
mum temperature of 114 degrees is reported
at Phoenix.

hours ending midnight June 24:
Northern California â Cloudy unsettled weath
er Tuesday: cooler and possibly light showers
in northern portion Tuesday; fresh southwest
winds.
Southern California â Fair Tuesday, continued
warm weather; light southerly winds.
Nevada â Cloudy Tuesday, cooler; brisk to
h!irh southwest winds.
San Francisco and vicinity â Fair Tuesday,
continued warm weather: brisk southerly wind.

of this gain was lost on the reaction.- . Among
the industrials Colorado Fuel was a conspicu
ous sufferer on account of the supposed dispute,
among insiders as to the continuance of divi
dends upon the stock. The recent heavy tone
of Amalgamated Copper also gave way to posi
tive weakness In view of the continued decline
in the price of copper and the unprecedented
production. Supporting orders seemed to be
withdrawn in United States Steel, and that
Mock suffered a net loss of nearly a point. It
is the general conviction in Wall street that
the proposed plan for conversion of preferred
stocks into 5 per cent bonds will be blocked
by the courts, thus -leaving no offset to the in
crease of fixed charges on the fifty million dol
lar new bond issue. There seems to be a fear
that the payment of dividends on the common
nocks is thus threatened. The reports of
minor labor troubles, while not directly affect-
Ing any special stocks, have a somewhat de
pressing effect on sentiment," and several re
ports of voluntary increases In wages instead
of ha\'lng a reassuring effect were taken as
pointing to the prevailing discontent of labor
with the present rate of pay. Saturday's un
satisfactory bank statement was borne in mind
Â¦to-day, and probably helped to repress specula
tion. The closing was easy at the considerable
reaction in railroad stocks. A number of the
lesa active stocks show large Â¦ losses. :

MAKES FIGHT WITH K^TIFE
BEFORE BEING ARRESTED
Frank Bickford Booked at the '.City
Prison on , Charge of ' -
Burglary.
Frank Rickford went into the Adama
lodging-house, at 639 Clay street, yester
day afternoon and after entering one of
the rooms locked the door so that hÂ«
cculd not be disturbed. W. J. McCreary.
a son of .the proprietor, saw him enter
and knocked on the door, demanding
what he wanted. Rickford kept quiet for
a few minutes, and as McCreary called
for some one to come and help him Rlck
fcrd opened the door. Rickfor.i had an
open .- knife in his hand, but McCrearv
grappled -with him. While they were
struggling assistance arrived and Rick
ford was cverpo-wered. --
Policeman J. F. O'Brien was summoned
and he took Rickford to the City Prison,
where he was booked on a charge of
burglary. The interior of the room
showed that Rickford had made a hur
ried search for valuables.

The Boara of Works has rejected" all
bids for the paving of Market street from
Van Ness avenue to Twelfth street with
bitumen rock on a concrete bed, and new
calls for bids were issued yesterday and
arc returnable on Saturday next. On the
original call six bids were returned, and
James H. Bingham would have been givea
the work had it not been that In his re
turns he bid 17^4 and omitted the, word
cents. In consequence of this error the
Board of Works has issued a new call,
and bids will be passed upon on June 28.
Board of Works Issues New Call.
