Philip J. Miller, assistant bartender In
T. J. Clancy's saloon, 710 Kearny street,
was booked at the City Prison yesterday
by Detectives" Dinsin and Wren on a
charge of grand larceny. Clancy notified
Captain Martin Sunday that $117 had been
stolen from a bag in his safe between
Saturday night . and Sunday morning.
Dlnan and Wren ' were ; detailed on the
case, and yesterday they found Miller
in his room at the Prescott House.
When searched he had $115 in his pocket,
which was booked as evidence.

Trouble Over Restaurant Deal.
Alfred Grayson, a soldier in the Six
teenth Infantry, which returned recently
from the Philippines on the transport
Sherman, was arrested last night and
charged with assault with a deadly weap
on. He got into an altercation with John
Colabaia, proprietor of the restaurant at
627 Kearny street, at about 11:30 o'clock.
Colabaia followed the soldier out on the
sidewalk and was having a tussle with
him when Grayson drew a revolver. Lieu
tenant Gleason, Sergeant Donovan and
Officer Mulcahy witnessed the fight and
took Grayson into custody.
Soldier Draws a Revolver.

A. D. Bowley, a curbstone broker, was
held to answer before the Superior Court
by Judge Mogan yesterday on a charge of
felony embezzlement. He is accused of
having embezzled 300 shares of the Cen
tral Eureka Mining Company, valued at
$315, which H. E. Averell, 240 Montgomery
street, gave him to sell on January 20
last. Bowley said he had traded the
shares for a diamond sunburst, which he
wanted Averell to take, but Averell re
fused.

The United States Civil Service Com
mission will on June 26 hold an examina
tion for the position of pressman, Phil
ippine service, two vacancies, salary $1400
per annum each. Age limit 18 years or
over. Applicants will be required to show
the extent of their experience with liiehle
presses. Persons who desire to compete
should apply to the United States Civil
Service Commission, Washington, D. C,
or to the secretary of the local board of
examiners, Postofnce, for application
form 1093, which should be filed with the
commission at Washington. Persons un
able to file applications but whose re
quests are received In time to ship papers
to the examination places will be exam
ined.

Programme of Unusual Merit Is Pre-
On Wednesday evening, the 25th Inst..
an entertainment will be given at the
Seamen's Institute, at 33 Steuart street,
and it promises to be one of the best ever
held there.
Professor Cadrel Hopkins will give a
piano solo and Harry Barbee will sing.
There will be a specialty by Charles Mat
thews and a solo by Miss May Pettee.
Lucian Bacigalupi will render a vocal solo
and Fred Bowman will give a monologue.
There will be a song by Harry P. Con
nor, and Robert C. Madison will show how
clever he is as a mimic. Miss Ruth Wil
son will dance and selections will be given
by Miss Flora Davis. There will be a spe
cialty by Al Sanders and a vocal solo by
Miss Gertrude Prather. At the conclusion
John Pettee will deliver an address.
T. Ota, second officer of the Japanese steam
ship Hongkong Mam, was waylaid by footpads
on Sunday morning In the vicinity of the Mail
dock, robbed and roughly handled. He spent
Saturday evening a guest of the Japanese Con
sul, and, later paid a visit to the Nippon Club,
where he remained until after midnight. He
was attacked about 200- yards from the dock.
His assailants first struck him over the head.
While one of them held him by the throat the
others stripped him of valuables. They took
his watch and his money, they ripped off his
collar and tore out the bosom of his shirt for
the Jeweled buttons displayed there, and" re
moved his cuffs which contained more jewelry.
They were pulling oft his shoes when the sound
of approaching footsteps scared them away.
The police are working on the case, but Ota
vows he will never again walk the San Fran
cisco streets after nightfall.
Footpads at Mail Dock.

The California Inanimate Target Asso
ciation met in the office of A. M. Shields.
Crocker building, last evening, for the
purpose of dissolving the association. The
meeting was called to order by the presi
dent, Dr. W. S. George of Antloch. The
affairs of the association were carefully
considered, the bills being all paid and the
surplus divided among the clubs in good
standing. A new association was then
formed with Dr. W. S. George as the
temporary president, W. J. Street tem
porary secretary, and T. L. Lewis tem
porary vice president. A committee of
four, composed of Dr. W. S. George, W.
S. Street, T. L. Lewis and A. M. Shields,
was appointed to draft bylaws. After
some consideration it was decided to call
the association the Pacific Coast Trap
shooting Association, and to invite all the
States of. the Pacific Coast to join the as
sociation. Although' the association is
made up of nearly all amateur shooters,
professional men will not be excluded
from the tournaments, of which there
will be not less than one a year. Prepara
tions are being made by the organization
to make the new association a Buccess,
and one able to compete with the gun clubs
of the East. The next meeting will be
held at the call of the committee, when
the bylaws will be passed upon.
Members of Pacific Coast Trap Shoot-
ing Association Convene
and Elect Officers.
The San Francisco councils of the
Royal Arcanum celebrated the silver an
niversary of the order by an entertain
ment in Golden Gate Hall last night. The
hall was crowded by members of the or
der and their friends', and these were en
tertained with the presentation of the
beautiful play entitled "The Littlest
Girl," which was very well received.
There were also songs by Miss Mignon
Judson and J. H. Davies, a brief address
and the reading of the supreme regent's
letter by G. Gunzendorfer, grand regent
of California. The programme closed
with a jolly little comedietta entitled
"Because He Loved Her So," written by
M. S. de Roco, a member of the order.
There was dancing for an hour.

tainment was composed of G. B. Tuley,
chairman; George W. Dixon, secretary;

Troubles of Brotherhood Carpenters
Will Reach Acute Stage Next
Week.
The Building Trades Council was sus
tained in its fight against the brother
hood of carpenters by the members of
Millmen's Union No. 423 last night. The
indorsement consisted in voting in favor
of a resolution declaring that unions af
filiated with the council shall belong to
no other central organization. The vote
stood 347 for and 4 asainst.
Carpenters' Union No. 483 of the broth
erhood met last night and reports were
received tending to show that the broth
ethood men were standing firm. Notice
was received that the stairbuilders would
stand by the brotherhood. All appears
in readiness for the fight of next week,
when it is expected that the contractors
will carry out their resolve not to recog
nize any carpenters who cannot furnish
working cards issued by the Building
Trades Council.
At a meeting of the Barbers' Union last
night it was decided that the new time
schedule shall- go into effect on August 2
instead of the 9th, as 'was at first pro
posed. The schedule provides for one
hour a day "less than at present. " Shops
are to close at 10 o'clock Saturday nights,
at 11 a. m. Sundays and at 9 o'clock p. m.
on days preceding holidays. The schedule
meets with the opposition of the master
barbers and trouble is likely to ensue.
MILLMEN INDORSE
BUILDING TRADES
The Board of Election Commissioners
met yesterday morning, with Commis
sioner Oliver Everett in the chair. The
Registrar reported that 17,000 voters had
registered to date, an increase of 8966
over the same period in 1900, due to keep
ing the office open In the evenings.
The contract for erecting booths for the
primary election was awarded to George
P. de Gear at $12 55 per house.
Carl T. Spelling, attorney for the Union
Labor party, filed a petition for a place
on the official ballot at the primary elec
tion to be held August 12, 1902. This peti
tion is the first presented by any political
party, and is sworn to by William P. De
lany, chairman of the party's executive
committee.
The candidates named in the petition to
be nominated by the convention of the
party are Railroad Commissioner, mem
ber of the Board of Equalization, Con
gressmen from Fourth and Fifth dis
tricts, members of the Assembly for the
eighteen Assembly districts; Senators
from the Eighteenth, Twentieth, Twenty
second and Twenty-fourth Senatorial dis
tricts; Judges of the Superior Court and
Justices of the Peace.
After hearing statements of Attorney
Spelling the formal consideration of the
petition was set for Monday, June 30, at 9
a. m.
Six hundred and ninety-eight election
officers were appointed for the 113 pre
cincts at the primary to be held August
12â one inspector, two. judges and three
clerks for each precinct.

Commissioners.

THE sad news was received in the
city yesterday that Theodore
Goodman Hosmer, a popular
young student of Stanford Uni
versity, had died on Sunday at
Wawona.
The young man was nearly 21 years of
age and had a promising future. He was
just entering the junior class at college.
He was attacked with appendicitis while
in the Yosemite and was taken to the
hotel at Wawona. Dr. Gardner of the
Southern Pacific was summoned from
this city and decided that an operation
was necessary. The young man passed
away soon after this operation.
J. A. Hosmer, who holds a responsible
position in" the general passenger office
of the Southern Pacific, was the father
of the deceased, and General Passenger
Agent T. H. Goodman of the same com
pany was his uncle. ''.
Last evening a number of friends of the
deceased left for Wawona and will escort
the body to Palo Alto, where the funeral
will take place, possibly on Thursday.
A pathetic Incident of the young man's
death is the fact that his mother, who Is
journeying from the East,, is not aware of
it. T. H. Goodman left last evening to
meet Mrs. Hosmer at Truckee and inform
her of the bereavement.
Theodore Q. Hosmer
Dies Suddenly at
Wawona.

Albert Peterson, 19 Hattie street, was
tried before Judge Mogan yesterday on
tho charge of battery upon his son Alfred,
11 years of age. The boy testified that
last Friday his father threw hi3 daughter
Ida, who is 6 years of age, on the bed
and commenced to choke her. The boy
rushed to his sister's assistance and the
defendant struck him and chased him. out
of the house. It was all because Ida had
worn her new dress that day. The boy,
In answer to the Judge, said that his
father always beat him and. his little sis
ters, and was drunk most of the time.
Peterson got permission to question the
boy and asked, "Who told you to tell
these lies about me?" and the little fel
low replied, "They are not lies. I am
speaking the truth, and you know It."
The boy also said that his father chased
his mother out of the house and threat
ened to kill her if she had him arrested.

Climatclo gists Visit Places of Inter
est and- Conclude Their Stay
With Banquet.
â¢ The Eastern climatologists who are vis
iting California in the interests of science
returned from their trip- to ; Mount Tamal
pais yesterday morning. They one "and
all declared that the view was the finest |
it had ever been their good fortune to I
enjoy. Forecaster McAdie of the Weath
er Bureau was their pilot on the excur
sion.
The visitors spent yesterday afternoon
at the park. Cliff, beach and other places
of interest. On their return they were
banqueted at a downtown restaurant.
The presidents of the local commercial
organizations, the prominent physicians
of the city and several professors from
the State and Stanford universities were
present at the feast and accorded the vis
itors a hearty welcome.
The party has concluded its researches
and will leave for the East to-day.

VALUABLE STATUE ARRIVES
IN NEW YOSK FROM ITALY
Joseph . D. Bedding's Beautiful Pur
chase, Ariadne, -to Be Shown
First in San Francisco.
A statue. of great beauty has just ar
rived in New York from' Florence, Italy,
and will in the course of a couple of
weeks be on exhibition at the Mark Hop
kins Art Institute..
The work of art was purchased by Jo
seph D. Redding while in Florence, and
though he was invited to give' a public
view of the statue in New York, Mr. Red
ding for the sentimental reason of having
bf en connected at one time with the Hop
kins Art Institute as president declined
to "have the object d'art shown elsewhere
than within the walls of the institute. -
The statue, which is life size and fun
length, represents Ariadne, tho wife of
Bacchus, goddess of the vine. The figure,
which is of Carrara marble, is lightly
draped and is holding aloft a goblet while
apparently coming down ' the vine-cla>i
hills of Sicily. '. . â¢
The sculptor is Professor, Bertolotti,
who is well known for the admirable
work he has done for the Bazzantls of
Florence. -. Â¦-' Â¦ â¢ '
Mr. Redding, Influenced with a desire
that his friends of California should be
the first to view "Ariadne," has also
been swayed in his wish to exhibit the
statue here by the idea that it wiir be a
valuable study for the students of Cali
fornia.

Mrs. Margaret Randall, Beloved of
the Veterans, Is Killed at Her
Residence by Gas.
OAKLAND, June 23.â Mrs. Margaret M.
Randall, known as Grandma Randall in
Grand Army circles throughout the Pa
cific Coast, was found dead from gas
suffocation this morning in her bedroom
at 1306 Harrison street. The discovery
was made by the aged woman's son,
George B. Randall, who had gone to tha
room to see why his mother had not risen.
There is not the slightest evidence of
suicide. According to the son, the old
lady, who was nearly 75 years of ag^.
retired last night in the best of health,
after enjoying a family reunion during
the day with her children and grandchil
dren.
Mrs. Randall's life had been one . of
much activity in religious and Grand
Army circles. During the Civil War she
was a volunteer army nurse In the hos
pitals at her native city, Albany, N. Y.
After the organization of the Grand Army
of the. Republic and the women's auxil
iaries Mrs. Randall became identified
very closely with that work. Through
out California and Oregon the veteran
nurse was well known. During the later
years of her life the appellation of
"Grandma" was given to her because of
her motherly Interest In the veterans'
'welfare. Mrs. Randall "was an honorary
member of John A. Dlx Circle, . Woman's
Relief Corps of San Jose. For four years
she had resided with her son, Mr. Ran
dall, in Alameda, Berkeley and in thla
city. Another son, Cuarles A. Gilbert,
also resides in Oakland. She was a
widow.
The Coroner will hold an inquest. ThÂ»
funeral will be held Wednesday afternoon
at 2 o'clock under Grand Army auspices
at Loring Hall. The interment will be
in the Grand' Army plat at Mountain
View Cemetery. ,.-,;Â¦- v .'^i-'

Used Scissors to Insure Silence.
An Italian peddler, who gave the. name. of
Patrick McHugh, was caught yesterday by
Police Sergeant Thomas Mahoney in the act
of cutting out the tongue of a goose. The
alleged McHueh explained that the goose re
fused to go along quietly, and as he had a pair
of scissors In his socket and intended to kill
the goose anyway just as soon as convenient,
he thought he could best insure the necessary
silence by removing the tongue. McHugh was
drunk, and as he had got no further with his
silencing operation than getting a focus on the
tongue, he was locked up on the plain charge
of drunk. â¢ Â¦ <â¢Â¦;-. - . â¢.

SAN JOSE, June 23.â San Francisco
Turn Verein captured the honors of the
nineteenth annual turnfest of the Pacific
Bezirk and Frederick Attinger, one of its
members, was declared the star Turner.

Oakland Turn Verein third and Sacra
mento Turn Verein fourth. The scores
made at Schuetzen Park to-day were all
good, although no records were broken.
The pole vaulting was above the average.

BERKELEY, June 23.â The use of fire
for the extermination of the grasshoppers
that are ravishing the fields of the State
is no longer of any account. So much
time has elapsed now since the pests be
gan their, depredations with little being
done to destroy them that wings have
grown upon them, providing them with
the means of flying from the approach of
fire. .

represented the Dominican order at the
altar.
Three of the young men took major or
ders and three minor orders. Those made
subdeacons were Brother Bertrand Clyno
of Benicia and Brother Humbert Kelly
and Brother Andrew Hunt of Sari Fran
cisco. Those ordained to the minor order
were Brother Raphael Corcoran of 'Val
lejo and Brother Basil Kenny and Brother
Antonious Lewis of this city.
This morning at 7 o'clock at the same
church the three former brothers will be
raised by the Archbishop to the rank of
deacons and on Wednesday they will be
ordained to the priesthood.
Next Monday morning at 11 o'clock
Brother Humbert Kelly will, as a Do
minican father, celebrate his first mass at
St. Dominic's Church. This being his
birthplace, it is expected that a large
number of his friends will attend the sac
rament. He will be assisted by Father
Wilson as deacon and Father Clancy as
subdeacon. Father Driscoll; will preach
the sermon on this occasion.

at the same time, will journey to' Benicia

Emil Nusbaurner, who is getting the
credit for compiling those incriminating
affidavits against Assessor Dalton and
Editor Daniels, was a witness before the
Grand Jury td-dav. Mr. Nusbaumer talk
ed right out in meeting and didn't mind
telling all about it afterward. Here are
some of the things he says he said:
"You know anything goes before a
Grand Jury. They take all kind of hear
say testimony and if a fellow feela like
making a stump speech' it's all right. So
I made my stump speech.
"They asked me if I knew what be
came of those missing books, and I told
them I didn't know, but that if they
wanted my opinion I would give it. Then,
without any invitation, I proceeded to tell
them that I was pretty . certain there
never were any books to miss and that
Daniels and Dalton got the money for
them.
"They asked me if I was instrumental
in bringing about these charges, and I
said to them: 'Just imagine your worst
enemy and then think of all the venom
that you have stored up against him for
years, and you will have some idea of
my position. These fellows have been
attacking me ruthlessly, brutally, unnec
essarily and I resent it." That is what I
said."

changes. Secretary Shaw has old-fash
ioned notions as to these matters, and if
the Department of Justice cannot find

jointly responsible for what amounted al
most to a miscarriage of justice, he is
likely to endeavor to find grounds for
tlieir removal.

Solicitor of the Treasury, United States
District Attorney of New York, chief of
special agents and the whole shoal of
smaller fry.

Secretary of the Treasury, has been defi
nitely notified by Secretary Shaw that he
i3 to retire. He will probably not again
exercise the functions of an assistant, tho
Secretary of the Treasury being at the
present time on a leave of absence. Gen
eral Spaulding has been in the customs
service for many years. He is a very ex
pert official, knowing all the intricate de
tails of tariffs and their application. His
position placed him in charge of all of the
intricate cases of disputed duties, and he,
as head of the customs division, opposed

had been evading customs auties to the

Although it is the Chinese language
which is spoken by the largest number of
people on the face of the earth, it is in
KnfcUsh that more than half pf all ex
isting newspapers are written." Against
a population of nearly 400,000,000 which
speak Chinese, English is spoken by
about 100,000,000. Next comes German with
85,fK>0,000, then Russian v with 65,000,000.
French and Spanish- are each the native
tongue of 41,000,000, Italian of 30,000,000 and
Portuguese of only 13,000,000. In the Unit
ed States newspapers appear printed in
twenty-four different languages. The Ital
ian tongue is, outside Italy, mainly spok
en in' Egypt and America. The use of
Spanish is decreasing, but is still a very
important language In commerce.âWest
minster Gazette. .

bers.

REAL ESTATE TRANSACTIONS.
It was decided to-night that the Eng
lish varsity men should meet the Har
vard and Yale boxers July 1.
The .betting started at 5 to ;| on Wal
cott. The attendance for this fight was
small. â¢ : :
Joe Walcott, the colored pugilist, won
his fight with Tommy West of Brooklyn
for the welter-weight championship on
points. This contest was a disappoint
ment to the spectators at the National
Sporting Club, as it lacked the vigor
which has characterized thÂ« Â¦ previous
meetings of these two men. VWalcott
fought for the body and did a deal of ef
fective work. He was/quicker in his
movements than his opponent and had
West in a poor plight jjuef ore the conclu
sion of the second round. â¢ After this
round he improved and landed come se
vere jabs on Walcott's mouth. The two
men were repeatedly in holds and the
referee was compelled to separate them.
In the latter "stages of the light West
fought gamely, but to no purpose. The
negro was tiring him out and waiting for
a chance to get in a finishing punch. This
opening nejver came, the bout lasting the
full fifteen rounds, and at the end Wal
cott was an easy winner on points.
The pace was' terrific throughout the
contest and McFadden made a resolute
fight, but Jordan was always a shade
better on . points, and - the referee would
have decided in his favor even if he had
not landed a knockout: blow.
The bout was held in connection with
the coronation spring, tournament, and
the fighters afforded the habitues of the
club an opportunity to witness a flna
struggle.- Jordan, the Englishman, start
ed in a pronounced favorite. It was any
body's fight up to the tenth round, when
Jordan showed prominently with a series
of blows on McFadden's body. ' In the
twelfth â¢ round McFadden Â¦ knocked- ' the
Englishman down, but the latter arose
apparentty unhurt. After this Jordan in
dulged in some tremendous hitting, and
fifteen seconds before the close of the lit
teenth round he knocked McFadden .out
with a fine cross; which landed clean on
the point of the jaw. McFad.len was
still unconscious at the end of the full
count. : . â¢" . . â¢' â¢ Â¦

"The Two Orphans," that ever popular

plause of the delighted spectators. The

and the varicolored lanterns swinging

act in- the play was set with similar care
and thoroughness, and that was the way
in which . the piece ,was played, too.

Mr. Miller has in Quex an apt vehi
cle for the display of his various dis
tinctions, finish, subtlety, manner, and
the rest. Ho is most grateful to the part
and the part to him, and Pinero's re
formed lord would go far. to find a more
finished interpreter. Sir. ' Chichestei
Frayne, Quex's'- unreformed" friend, is
delightfully assumed by Lawrence D'Or
say. and Miss Eustace gives a highly sym
pathetic portrait of the Duchess. Grace
Elliston is pretty in the pretty role of
Muriel, and tho rest of the cast are all
competent. The piece. It need not be said,
is mounted splendidly, the garden scene
at Fauncey Court being a de luxe edition
of the kindf'of thing.
the contrast is stunningly effective. Both
finally reach a common ground, and are
betrayed into a mutual admiration that
is as esthetically grotesque as their for
mer dislike. The last act, that reads
rather dully after the third, acts delight
fuliy, and but' for a slight sag in the sec
ond act there is no. lag in the interest. .
One trembles for "The Gay Lord Quex"
in the hands of any but the best of com
panies, but there was no tremble com
ing last night. Possibly to Miss Anglin
belongs thechiefest honor of the- illusion,
for her completely convincing assumption
of the manicurist role, so radically out; of
key with anything in her former reper
toire.* She invested the part with a rich,
unconscious humor." a "snippy," . flippant,
smart, impudent, yet sound-hearted at
mosphere, that set Sophy before one with
the strong, droll line of a Phil May.
Her gesture, tone, glance, everything,
strengthened the illusion, and Miss An
glin's Fullgarney will go down as one of
the greatest comedy portraits of the pe
riod. . . %

CONSULTATION IS HELD.
The complaints filed against Black by
Colonel Andrews of the Diamond Palace
and by Cashier Scott of the "Western Na
tional Bank were based on checks drawn
by the accused on the Bank of Santa
Clara. The checks drawn by Black were
returned and protested and complaint was
made to the police.
Black had deposited a genuine' draft
with the Santa Clara bank, but he was
requested by that institution not to draw
against the draft until word came from
the East that the document was good.
Black's haste to secure funds proved his
undoing. Before the Santa Clara bank
heard from the East, Black's checks were
presented and protested.
Word came from Indianapolis yesterday
that Black's- drafts deposited with the
Santa Clara .bank had been paid. Conse
quently Colonel Andrews and the "West
ern National bank will receive their mon
ey from the Santa Clara, bank on Black's
checks.'
The draft deposited by Black in the
name of A. Young, for the sum of $1000,
with the National Bank of Oakland, .has
been paid also. This draft was drawn on
.the National Bank' of Indianapolis in fa
vor of "J. B. Washington.'.'
The Oakland bank received advices yes
terday from the bank In Indianapolis
stating that while the draft was good,
there was something peculiar in the trans
action. The Indianapolis bank officials
stated that J "J. B. Washington" had
drawn b.ut one draft - against them and
that he had checks printed with his name
and also that of the Indianapolis bank as
hi& correspondent.'
From the Oakland Bank Black drew j
the :,sum of $600 by check, and deposited
the same with the Bank of California,
signing his name as "G. W. Pratt." Black
also deposited $1000 In cash with the Bank
of California, and subsequently drew all
the money out with the exception of $60.
In the faco of the genuine drafts depos
ited by Black the complaints filed against
him last week will : fall to the ground,
but the prisoner will not be given ills lib
erty. The police, of Springfield. Mass.,
want him for passing a forged check for
$385. â¢;â¢Â¦;, -

CHECKS WILL BE PAID.
The' swift action of the police depart
ment of this city nipped in the bud the
daring schemes of the man of many
aliases. If he had not- been arrested last
week, .his- credit would have been good
with . many banking institutions in this
and other cities.
The payment of some of the drafts and
checks is looked upon by the police^ as
being a part of the plans of Black to
swindle banks all over the country. -Hav
ing once established his credit with a
bank. It would be an easy matter for
Black to deposit forged or worthless
drafts and checks and araw against them
before advice could be received from the
East. . :
Although information reached the police
yesterday that some of, the drafts and
checks deposited by Black in California
banks have been paid in the East since
his arrest. Captain of Detectives Martin
has every hope that Black will be given a
long term "in prison.
